Question: Email investigation
Answer:
Answer:
**Email Investigation ‚Äì End‚Äëto‚ÄëEnd Guide (UAE Federal Decree‚ÄëLaw No.‚ÄØ35/2022, Articles‚ÄØ9‚Äë12)**  

Below is a practical, step‚Äëby‚Äëstep framework you can follow when you are tasked with investigating an email (or a set of emails) for evidentiary or forensic purposes. The workflow is organized into three major phases ‚Äì **Legal/Procedural, Technical Acquisition & Preservation, and Analytical Review** ‚Äì and includes the specific tools, artefacts, and documentation you should capture to satisfy both the technical rigor and the legal admissibility requirements under UAE law.

---

## 1Ô∏è‚É£ LEGAL & PROCEDURAL PRE‚ÄëREQUISITES  

| Action | Why it matters | How to document |
|--------|----------------|-----------------|
| **Obtain lawful authority** ‚Äì court order, subpoena, or explicit user consent (written, signed, dated). | Guarantees the acquisition is ‚Äúcourt‚Äësanctioned‚Äù per Art.‚ÄØ9‚Äë12. | Keep a copy of the order/consent in the case file; note the issuing authority, case number, and date. |
| **Define scope** ‚Äì specific mailbox(es), date range, sender/recipient filters, and any related accounts (e.g., shared mailboxes, aliases). | Prevents over‚Äëcollection and protects privacy. | Draft a ‚ÄúScope of Collection‚Äù memo signed by the investigator and the authorizing officer. |
| **Chain‚Äëof‚ÄëCustody (CoC) plan** ‚Äì assign a custodian, log every hand‚Äëoff, and use tamper‚Äëevident containers (digital hash‚Äësealed archives). | Required for admissibility; shows evidence was not altered. | Use a CoC spreadsheet or a dedicated e‚Äëdiscovery platform (e.g., Relativity, Logikcull). |
| **Data protection & confidentiality** ‚Äì comply with UAE PDPL (Personal Data Protection Law) when handling personal data. | Avoids civil liability and evidentiary challenges. | Mark all exported files with ‚ÄúConfidential ‚Äì PDPL‚ÄëCompliant‚Äù. Store on encrypted drives (AES‚Äë256). |

---

## 2Ô∏è‚É£ TECHNICAL ACQUISITION & PRESERVATION  

### 2.1 Choose the Right Collection Method  

| Method | When to use | How to execute |
|--------|-------------|----------------|
| **Live mailbox export (IMAP/POP/Exchange Web Services)** | Active accounts, need full folder hierarchy, including hidden items (e.g., drafts, deleted items). | ‚Ä¢ Use **Microsoft Outlook** ‚ÄúExport to .pst‚Äù (ensure ‚ÄúInclude subfolders‚Äù and ‚ÄúExport hidden items‚Äù). <br>‚Ä¢ For Exchange/Office‚ÄØ365, run **PowerShell**: <br>`New‚ÄëComplianceSearch ‚ÄìName "Case123" ‚ÄìExchangeLocation user@domain.com ‚ÄìContentMatchQuery 'Received:>2023‚Äë01‚Äë01'` <br>`Start‚ÄëComplianceSearch ‚ÄìIdentity "Case123"` <br>`Export‚ÄëComplianceSearch ‚ÄìIdentity "Case123"` |
| **Forensic image of mail server** | When the server is under your control (on‚Äëpremises) or you have admin rights. | Use **FTK Imager** or **dd** to create a bit‚Äëfor‚Äëbit image of the mail store (e.g., *.edb for Exchange). |
| **Third‚Äëparty e‚Äëdiscovery platform** | Large‚Äëscale investigations, need deduplication, early case assessment. | Upload raw .pst/.mbox files to **Logikcull**, **Relativity**, or **Everlaw** ‚Äì they automatically compute SHA‚Äë256 hashes. |
| **Client‚Äëside acquisition (mobile/desktop)** | When the user‚Äôs device holds local copies (e.g., Outlook OST, Apple Mail). | Use **Magnet AXIOM**, **Cellebrite UFED**, or **FTK** to acquire the local mail store. |

### 2.2 Preserve Integrity  

1. **Hash every artefact** immediately after acquisition (SHA‚Äë256 is the de‚Äëfacto standard).  
   ```bash
   sha256sum email_export.pst > email_export.pst.sha256
   ```
2. **Write‚Äëonce, read‚Äëmany (WORM) storage** ‚Äì store the original

Question: Extract the text present in the png file
Answer:
Answer:
Below is a concise, step‚Äëby‚Äëstep workflow you can follow to pull **any visible or hidden text** out of a PNG image.  
The approach covers the most common places where text can hide in a PNG:

| Where the text can live | Typical tool(s) | What the tool does |
|--------------------------|----------------|--------------------|
| **Standard metadata** (EXIF, XMP, PNG tEXt/zTXt/iTXt chunks) | `exiftool`, `pngcheck`, `exiftool -a -G1 -s <file>` | Reads all PNG text chunks and prints them in a readable form. |
| **Embedded files / steganographic payloads** | `steghide`, `zsteg`, `stegsolve`, `binwalk`, `strings` | Tries to uncover data hidden in LSBs, ancillary chunks, or appended payloads. |
| **OCR on the visual image** (if the text is rendered in the picture) | `tesseract`, `ocrmypdf` (for PNG ‚Üí PDF ‚Üí OCR) | Optical‚Äëcharacter‚Äërecognition to read printed/handwritten text. |
| **Hidden scripts / polyglot payloads** | `olevba` (via `oletools`), `detect-it-easy`, `file` | Detects embedded code that may masquerade as an image. |
| **Raw byte search** | `strings`, `grep -a`, `hexdump` | Pulls any printable ASCII/Unicode sequences that may be tucked anywhere in the file. |

Below is a **ready‚Äëto‚Äërun Bash script** (Linux/macOS) that strings together the most useful commands. Adjust the paths if you‚Äôre on Windows (PowerShell equivalents are provided after the script).

---

## 1Ô∏è‚É£ Quick‚Äëlook with `exiftool`

```bash
# Install (if needed)
#   brew install exiftool   # macOS
#   sudo apt-get install libimage-exiftool-perl   # Debian/Ubuntu

# Dump every text chunk
exiftool -a -G1 -s -s -s -TextualData your_image.png
```

*What you‚Äôll see*: All `tEXt`, `zTXt`, `iTXt`, `Comment`, `Description`, `Author`, etc. If the PNG contains a hidden comment, it will appear here.

---

## 2Ô∏è‚É£ Verify PNG integrity & list chunks

```bash
# Install pngcheck if you don‚Äôt have it
#   brew install pngcheck   # macOS
#   sudo apt-get install pngcheck   # Debian/Ubuntu

pngcheck -v your_image.png
```

`pngcheck` prints every chunk name (`IHDR`, `IDAT`, `tEXt`, `zTXt`, `iTXt`, `eXIf`, ‚Ä¶). If you spot an unfamiliar chunk (e.g., `sTEG`, `raW `), that‚Äôs a clue for steganography.

---

## 3Ô∏è‚É£ Extract *any* printable strings

```bash
strings -a -n 4 your_image.png > strings.txt
# -a : scan the whole file, not just the data section
# -n 4 : only keep strings of length ‚â•4 (adjust as needed)
```

Open `strings.txt` and look for:

* URLs, email addresses, command‚Äëline snippets, Base64 blobs, etc.
* Repeated patterns that could be a hidden payload.

---

## 4Ô∏è‚É£ Search for **Base64‚Äëencoded** blobs (common for hidden scripts)

```bash
grep -Eo '([A-Za-z0-9+/]{20,}={0,2})' strings.txt | sort -u > possible_b64.txt
```

If you find a long Base64 string, decode it:

```bash
base64 -d possible_b64.txt > decoded_payload.bin
file decoded_payload.bin   # see what it actually is
```

---

## 5Ô∏è‚É£ Steganography checks

### 5.1 LSB / palette‚Äëbased hiding (`zsteg`)

```bash
# Install
#   gem install zsteg   # Ruby gem
zsteg -a your_image.png
```

`zsteg` automatically tries a battery of LSB, color‚Äë

---

## 1Ô∏è‚É£ Extract the Urdu text (OCR)

| Tool | How to use | Why it works |
|------|------------|--------------|
| **Tesseract OCR** (open‚Äësource) | ```bash<br>tesseract image.png stdout -l urd<br>``` | Supports Urdu (`urd`) out‚Äëof‚Äëthe‚Äëbox. |
| **Google Cloud Vision API** | Upload the PNG via the console or call the API with `imageContext.languageHints = ["ur"]`. | Very high accuracy, handles complex fonts and mixed‚Äëlanguage images. |
| **Microsoft Azure Computer Vision** | Set `language = "ur"` in the request. | Good for printed and handwritten Urdu. |
| **Online OCR services** (e.g.,‚ÄØ[OnlineOCR.net](https://www.onlineocr.net/),‚ÄØ[OCR.Space](https://ocr.space/)) | Choose ‚ÄúUrdu‚Äù as the language and upload the file. | No installation needed; quick for one‚Äëoff jobs. |

**Tip:** If the image contains both Urdu and English (or other scripts), run OCR twice‚Äîonce with `-l urd` and once with `-l eng`‚Äîand then merge the results.

---

## 2Ô∏è‚É£ Clean up the OCR output (optional)

OCR can introduce stray characters, especially with right‚Äëto‚Äëleft scripts. A quick clean‚Äëup can be done with a small script:

```python
import re

def clean_urdu(text):
    # Remove common OCR artefacts (extra spaces, stray punctuation)
    text = re.sub(r'\s+', ' ', text)          # collapse whitespace
    text = re.sub(r'[^\w\s\u0600-\u06FF]', '', text)  # keep only Urdu letters & numbers
    return text.strip()

raw = """<paste‚Äëyour‚ÄëOCR‚Äëoutput‚Äëhere>"""
print(clean_urdu(raw))
```

| **DeepL API** (supports Urdu via ‚Äúauto‚Äù) | ```bash<br>curl -X POST "https://api.deepl.com/v2/translate" \ -d text="YOUR_URDU_TEXT" -d target_lang=EN -d auth_key=YOUR_KEY<br>``` | Often produces more natural‚Äësounding English. |

### How you can extract the text yourself

If you‚Äôd like to pull the text out of the PNG on your own machine, here are a few quick‚Äëand‚Äëeasy options:

| Tool | How to use | Notes |
|------|------------|-------|
| **Tesseract OCR** (open‚Äësource) | 1. Install Tesseract (`sudo apt-get install tesseract-ocr` on Linux, `brew install tesseract` on macOS, or download the Windows installer).<br>2. Run `tesseract image.png output -l urd` (the `-l urd` flag tells Tesseract to use the Urdu language pack).<br>3. The extracted text will be in `output.txt`. | Very accurate when you also install the Urdu language data (`tesseract-ocr-urd`). |
| **Google Drive / Google Docs** | 1. Upload the PNG to Google Drive.<br>2. Right‚Äëclick ‚Üí ‚ÄúOpen with‚Äù ‚Üí ‚ÄúGoogle Docs‚Äù.<br>3. Google Docs automatically runs OCR and places the recognized text below the image. | Works well for clear, high‚Äëresolution images; supports many languages including Urdu. |
| **Online OCR services** | ‚Ä¢‚ÄØ[OnlineOCR.net](https://www.onlineocr.net/)<br>‚Ä¢‚ÄØ[OCR.Space](https://ocr.space/)<br>Upload the PNG, select ‚ÄúUrdu‚Äù as the language, and download the result. | No installation needed, but be mindful of privacy if the image contains sensitive data. |
| **Microsoft OneNote** | 1. Insert the image into a OneNote page.<br>2. Right‚Äëclick the image ‚Üí ‚ÄúCopy Text from Picture‚Äù.<br>3. Paste the copied text elsewhere. | Works surprisingly well for many scripts, including Urdu. |
| **Mobile apps** | ‚Ä¢‚ÄØ**Google Lens** (Android/iOS) ‚Äì point the camera at the screen or open the saved image and tap ‚ÄúText‚Äù.<br>‚Ä¢‚ÄØ**Adobe Scan** ‚Äì can OCR Urdu if you set the language. | Handy when you‚Äôre on a phone or tablet. |

#### Quick tip for better OCR results
- **Resolution**: Aim for at least 300‚ÄØdpi. If the PNG is low‚Äëresolution, try up‚Äëscaling it a bit (e.g., with Photoshop or GIMP) before running OCR.
- **Contrast**: Increase contrast and convert to grayscale if the background is noisy.
- **Language pack**: Make sure the OCR engine has the Urdu language data installed; otherwise it will treat the characters as unknown symbols.

üîê Imaging and Hashing:
The first step in the examination process is creating a forensic image of the original device. This is a bit-for-bit copy that ensures the integrity of the data remains intact. Hash values (digital fingerprints) are generated for both the original and the forensic copy to prove that the data has not been altered.

Checklist for Imaging and Hashing:

Create a full forensic image of the device or media.

Calculate hash values (e.g., MD5, SHA1) for both the original and forensic image.

Verify the image‚Äôs integrity using the calculated hash values.

üìÅ File System & Metadata Analysis:
Forensic experts examine the device‚Äôs file system structure, looking at file names, directories, storage locations, and the metadata associated with files (such as creation and modification times). Metadata analysis can reveal important information about user actions and device usage, such as the timeline of events leading up to the crime.

| **2. | **3. 3.7 File Metadata & Hash Verification

Electronic files (images, PDFs, videos) are admissible under Decree-Law No. 35/2022, provided their integrity is proven. Metadata like creation date, last accessed time, geotag, and author can link suspects to files. Hashing ensures files have not been tampered with.

Checklist: Legal Compliance & Investigation Steps

‚úÖ Extract metadata from all evidentiary files (Exif, properties)

‚úÖ Generate SHA-256 or SHA-512 hash of file at first point of contact

‚úÖ Record details in chain of custody log

‚úÖ Validate no change in hash during storage and analysis

‚úÖ Attach forensic analyst certificate for integrity

Digital devices are core evidence sources. Under Article 36 of Decree-Law No. 34/2021, devices must be seized legally and processed using certified tools. Legal admissibility under Decree-Law No. 35/2022 depends on chain of custody, imaging methods, and handling logs.

3.7 File Metadata & Hash Verification

Electronic files (images, PDFs, videos) are admissible under Decree-Law No. 35/2022, provided their integrity is proven. Metadata like creation date, last accessed time, geotag, and author can link suspects to files. Hashing ensures files have not been tampered with.

3.8 Device Forensics (Mobile, Laptop, HDD)

Digital devices are core evidence sources. Under Article 36 of Decree-Law No. 34/2021, devices must be seized legally and processed using certified tools. Legal admissibility under Decree-Law No. 35/2022 depends on chain of custody, imaging methods, and handling logs.

üîê Imaging and Hashing:
The first step in the examination process is creating a forensic image of the original device. This is a bit-for-bit copy that ensures the integrity of the data remains intact. Hash values (digital fingerprints) are generated for both the original and the forensic copy to prove that the data has not been altered.

Checklist for Imaging and Hashing:

Create a full forensic image of the device or media.

Calculate hash values (e.g., MD5, SHA1) for both the original and forensic image.

Verify the image‚Äôs integrity using the calculated hash values.

üìÅ File System & Metadata Analysis:
Forensic experts examine the device‚Äôs file system structure, looking at file names, directories, storage locations, and the metadata associated with files (such as creation and modification times). Metadata analysis can reveal important information about user actions and device usage, such as the timeline of events leading up to the crime.

3.3 Email Communication

Emails are governed by Articles 9‚Äì12 of the Federal Decree-Law No. 35/2022. If extracted properly, with full headers and verified metadata, emails can serve as digital documentary evidence in court. The authenticity of sender identity, timestamps, and content integrity must be proven with hash values and server logs.

Checklist: Legal Compliance & Investigation Steps

‚úÖ Retrieve emails using court-sanctioned access or user consent

‚úÖ Ensure full headers (Received, IPs, user-agent) are extracted

‚úÖ Generate hash values for content integrity

‚úÖ Document email flow and source server with timestamp log

‚úÖ Secure Certificate of Authenticity under Article 11

3.4 IP Address Tracing

Question: Email investigation
Answer:
Answer:
Below is a **complete, step‚Äëby‚Äëstep playbook** you can use to investigate any suspicious e‚Äëmail ‚Äì from extracting the raw headers to proving the authenticity of the message in a court of law (UAE‚ÄØFederal Decree‚ÄëLaw No.‚ÄØ35/2022).  
All the tools you listed are incorporated, and each step notes **what to look for**, **why it matters**, and **the exact online service** you can use.

---

## 1Ô∏è‚É£  Acquire the Evidence (First‚Äëhand, Forensically Sound)

| Action | How to do it | Legal / Forensic notes |
|--------|--------------|------------------------|
| **Export the full e‚Äëmail (including all headers)** | ‚Ä¢ In Outlook ‚Üí ‚ÄúFile ‚Üí Save As ‚Üí .msg‚Äù  <br>‚Ä¢ In Gmail ‚Üí ‚ÄúShow original‚Äù ‚Üí download raw source  <br>‚Ä¢ In Thunderbird ‚Üí ‚ÄúView ‚Üí Message Source‚Äù ‚Üí copy | Keep the original file **read‚Äëonly**. Compute a SHA‚Äë256 hash immediately (e.g., `certutil -hashfile email.msg SHA256`). Store the hash in a separate, tamper‚Äëevident log. |
| **Document chain of custody** | Fill a simple template: date/time of extraction, device, user, method, hash, who performed the extraction, who received it. | Required by Article‚ÄØ11 (Certificate of Authenticity) and for admissibility in UAE courts. |
| **Obtain court‚Äëordered or consent‚Äëbased access** | If the mailbox is on a corporate server, request a **mutual legal assistance (MLA)** request or a **court order** before pulling logs. | Guarantees compliance with Articles‚ÄØ9‚Äë12. |

---

## 2Ô∏è‚É£  Parse & Analyse the Header

| Tool | What to feed it | What you‚Äôll get | Key indicators |
|------|----------------|----------------|----------------|
| **Header Analyzer Pro** (https://www.header-analyzer.com/) | Paste the raw header text | Visual ‚ÄúReceived‚Äù chain, SPF/DKIM/DMARC results, timestamps, IPs | Look for gaps, mismatched timestamps, ‚Äúunknown‚Äù hops. |
| **Microsoft Message Header Analyzer** (https://mha.azurewebsites.net/) | Same | Same as above, plus a ‚ÄúMessage Trace‚Äù view | Quick visual of the relay path. |
| **Google Header Analyzer** (toolbox.googleapps.com) | Same | SPF/DKIM/DMARC pass/fail, ‚ÄúAuthentication‚ÄëResults‚Äù | Spot failures that hint at spoofing. |
| **MxToolbox Email Headers** (https://mxtoolbox.com/EmailHeaders.aspx) | Same | Detailed ‚ÄúReceived‚Äù line breakdown, DNS lookups for each hop | Verify each hop‚Äôs IP resolves to the claimed host. |
| **Mailhardener Email Validator** (https://www.mailhardener.com/emailtools/email-analyzer) | Same | Reputation score, black‚Äëlist checks, SPF/DKIM/DMARC summary | Quick ‚Äúgood/bad‚Äù flag. |

**What to extract**

| Field | Why it matters | Typical red‚Äëflags |
|-------|----------------|-------------------|
| `Received:` lines | Shows every server that handled the mail, in reverse order. | ‚Ä¢ Missing hops <br>‚Ä¢ Private IPs (10.x, 192.168.x) in public path <br>‚Ä¢ Time‚Äëzone mismatches |
| `X‚ÄëOriginating‚ÄëIP` / `X‚ÄëClient-IP` | Direct sender IP (if present). | IP belongs to a known proxy/VPN or to a different country than claimed. |
| `Message‚ÄëID` | Unique identifier; can be cross‚Äëchecked with other messages. | Reused IDs across unrelated campaigns. |
| `From:` & `Reply‚ÄëTo:` | Claimed sender address. | Different domains, or ‚Äúdisplay name‚Äù that doesn‚Äôt match address. |
| `Authentication‚ÄëResults:` | SPF/DKIM/DMARC outcome. | Any **fail** or **softfail** ‚Üí possible spoof. |
| `Date:` | Timestamp + timezone. | Discrepancy with Received timestamps (e.g., Date says ‚Äú+02:00‚Äù but first hop shows ‚Äú+00:00‚Äù). |

| Tool | Command | What it does |
|------|---------|--------------|
| `file` (built‚Äëin) | `file your_image.png` | Confirms the file really is a PNG (checks magic bytes). |
| `md5sum` / `sha256sum` | `md5sum your_image.png`  <br> `sha256sum your_image.png` | Generates hash values you can later compare to the original source. |
| `binwalk` | `binwalk -e your_image.png` | Scans for embedded files/archives and extracts them automatically. |

**Result you‚Äôll get:** Confirmation that the file is a PNG, its hash, and any embedded payloads that binwalk can spot (e.g., ZIP archives, other image formats, executable blobs).

---

## 2Ô∏è‚É£ Pull out all EXIF / PNG‚Äëspecific metadata  

| Tool | Command | What it does |
|------|---------|--------------|
| **ExifTool** | `exiftool -a -u -g1 your_image.png > exif.txt` | Dumps every piece of metadata (including non‚Äëstandard PNG chunks). |
| **pngcheck** (optional) | `pngcheck -v your_image.png` | Shows PNG chunk structure (IHDR, PLTE, tEXt, iTXt, zTXt, etc.). |
| **Metadata2Go** (online) | Upload the file to <https://www.metadata2go.com/> | Gives a quick web‚Äëbased view of the same data. |

**What to look for:**  
- `tEXt` / `iTXt` / `zTXt` chunks that may contain hidden strings, URLs, or scripts.  
- Creation/Modification timestamps that differ from the filesystem timestamps.  
- Any custom chunks (e.g., `cHRM`, `gAMA`, `sRGB`, `bKGD`) that could be used for steganographic payloads.

---

## 3Ô∏è‚É£ Search for steganography / hidden payloads  

| Tool | Command | What it does |
|------|---------|--------------|
| **Stegdetect** (for JPEG, but works on PNG too) | `stegdetect -v your_image.png` | Quick heuristic scan for common stego tools. |
| **zsteg** (Ruby) | `zsteg -a your_image.png` | Checks LSB, LSB‚ÄëR, and other bit‚Äëplane tricks. |
| **Steghide** (if you suspect a password‚Äëprotected payload) | `steghide extract -sf your_image.png` | Prompts for a passphrase; will tell you if a hidden archive is present. |
| **stegsolve** (GUI) | Open the PNG in Stegsolve ‚Üí cycle through ‚ÄúRed‚Äù, ‚ÄúGreen‚Äù, ‚ÄúBlue‚Äù, ‚ÄúAlpha‚Äù, ‚ÄúLSB‚Äù, ‚ÄúBit‚Äëplane‚Äù etc. | Visual inspection of each channel/plane for hidden images or text. |
| **binwalk** (again) | `binwalk -E your_image.png` | `-E` forces extraction of embedded files even if they‚Äôre hidden in non‚Äëstandard chunks. |

**Typical findings:**  
- LSB‚Äëencoded text or another image.  
- A ZIP/7z archive tucked inside a custom PNG chunk.  
- A QR code or other visual steganographic element (e.g., a tiny barcode in the corner).

3.3 Email Communication

Emails are governed by Articles 9‚Äì12 of the Federal Decree-Law No. 35/2022. If extracted properly, with full headers and verified metadata, emails can serve as digital documentary evidence in court. The authenticity of sender identity, timestamps, and content integrity must be proven with hash values and server logs.

Checklist: Legal Compliance & Investigation Steps

‚úÖ Retrieve emails using court-sanctioned access or user consent

‚úÖ Ensure full headers (Received, IPs, user-agent) are extracted

‚úÖ Generate hash values for content integrity

‚úÖ Document email flow and source server with timestamp log

‚úÖ Secure Certificate of Authenticity under Article 11

3.4 IP Address Tracing

Question: email investigation
Answer:
Answer:
Below is a **complete, step‚Äëby‚Äëstep playbook** you can use to investigate any suspicious e‚Äëmail ‚Äì from locating the originating IP address and geolocation to validating the sender, checking for spoofing, and tying the message to known threat actors or campaigns.  
The workflow is organized into four logical phases:

| Phase | Goal | Core Activities | Primary Tools |
|-------|------|----------------|---------------|
| **1Ô∏è‚É£ Capture & Preserve** | Secure the evidence in a forensically‚Äësound way | ‚Ä¢ Export the full raw message (including all headers) <br>‚Ä¢ Generate cryptographic hash (SHA‚Äë256) of the .eml file <br>‚Ä¢ Store the .eml and hash in a tamper‚Äëevident repository | Outlook/Thunderbird ‚ÄúSave As‚ÄØ.eml‚Äù, **HashMyFiles**, **Open‚ÄëSource Forensic Toolkit (OSFT)** |
| **2Ô∏è‚É£ Header & IP Analysis** | Identify the real sending server(s) and geolocation | ‚Ä¢ Parse Received‚Äë* headers <br>‚Ä¢ Build a relay chain timeline (timestamp ‚Üí timezone) <br>‚Ä¢ Resolve each hop‚Äôs IP ‚Üí ISP ‚Üí location <br>‚Ä¢ Detect anomalies (missing hops, internal private IPs, mismatched timezones) | **Header Analyzer Pro**, **Microsoft Message Header Analyzer**, **MxToolbox Email Headers**, **Google Header Analyzer**, **IPinfo.io**, **WhatIsMyIPAddress** |
| **3Ô∏è‚É£ Sender & Domain Reputation** | Verify that the address/domain is legitimate and not a disposable/compromised service | ‚Ä¢ Extract the envelope‚Äëfrom and From: address <br>‚Ä¢ Check if the domain is a known free‚Äëmail provider, disposable service, or custom corporate domain <br>‚Ä¢ Run domain reputation scans (malware, phishing, spam) <br>‚Ä¢ Look for prior data‚Äëbreach exposure | **EmailHippo**, **MailTester**, **VirusTotal**, **Cisco Talos**, **MailCheck.ai**, **EmailListVerify**, **Hunter**, **LinkedIn** |
| **4Ô∏è‚É£ Behavioral & Threat‚ÄëIntel Correlation** | Determine whether the message is part of a larger phishing/scam campaign | ‚Ä¢ Scan subject line, body, URLs, attachments for phishing signatures <br>‚Ä¢ Compare writing style/grammar to known phishing templates <br>‚Ä¢ Query open‚Äësource threat‚Äëintel platforms for IOCs (IP, domain, hash, URL) <br>‚Ä¢ Map to MITRE ATT&CK techniques and known threat actors | **PhishTool**, **Mailhardener Email Validator**, **Scamwatch**, **FraudWatch**, **MITRE ATT&CK**, **Maltego**, **VirusTotal (IOC search)** |

## üìÅ 1Ô∏è‚É£ Capture & Preserve the Email

1. **Obtain the raw message**  
   - In Outlook: *File ‚Üí Save As ‚Üí .eml*  
   - In Gmail (Web): open the message ‚Üí click the three‚Äëdot menu ‚Üí **Show original** ‚Üí download the raw source.  

2. **Generate a hash** (SHA‚Äë256 is standard for legal admissibility).  
   ```bash
   certutil -hashfile suspicious.eml SHA256
   ```
   Record the hash in your case log and store the .eml in a write‚Äëonce, read‚Äëonly (WORM) storage location.

3. **Document the chain of custody** ‚Äì who collected the email, when, on what device, and under what authority (court order, user consent, MLA, etc.).  

---

## üîé 2Ô∏è‚É£ Header & IP Analysis

### 2.1 Extract the full header block
```
Received: from mail.example.com (mail.example.com. [203.0.113.45])
        by mx.google.com with ESMTPS id x7si1234567qka.123.2025.01.01.12.34.56
        for <victim@company.com>;
        Wed, 01 Jan 2025 12:34:56 -0800 (PST)
...
```

| Hop # | Received‚Äë* line | IP address | Hostname (PTR) | ISP / Org | Geo‚Äëlocation | Timestamp (UTC) | Timezone offset |

üîê Imaging and Hashing:
The first step in the examination process is creating a forensic image of the original device. This is a bit-for-bit copy that ensures the integrity of the data remains intact. Hash values (digital fingerprints) are generated for both the original and the forensic copy to prove that the data has not been altered.

Checklist for Imaging and Hashing:

Create a full forensic image of the device or media.

Calculate hash values (e.g., MD5, SHA1) for both the original and forensic image.

Verify the image‚Äôs integrity using the calculated hash values.

üìÅ File System & Metadata Analysis:
Forensic experts examine the device‚Äôs file system structure, looking at file names, directories, storage locations, and the metadata associated with files (such as creation and modification times). Metadata analysis can reveal important information about user actions and device usage, such as the timeline of events leading up to the crime.

Answer:
**Detailed Summary of the Extracted PNG Text**

The document outlines a step‚Äëby‚Äëstep forensic workflow and the associated tools for analyzing various types of digital evidence. It is organized into several sections, each focusing on a specific area of investigation.

---

### 1. Imaging & Hashing
- **Purpose:** Create a bit‚Äëfor‚Äëbit forensic copy of the original device/media to preserve evidence integrity.
- **Key Actions:**
  1. Produce a full forensic image.
  2. Generate hash values (e.g., MD5, SHA‚Äë1) for both the original source and the forensic image.
  3. Compare the hashes to verify that the copy is identical and unaltered.
- **Outcome:** Guarantees that any later analysis is performed on an exact replica, with a verifiable chain of custody.

---

### 2. File System & Metadata Analysis
- **Focus:** Examine the structure of the file system and the metadata attached to files (creation, modification timestamps, etc.).
- **Why It Matters:** Metadata can reveal user actions, device usage patterns, and a timeline of events leading up to an incident.
- **Tool Mentioned:** **Olevba** (part of the Oletools suite) ‚Äì used for extracting and analyzing OLE‚Äëbased metadata.
- **Reference:** https://github.com/decalage2/oletools/wiki/olevba

---

### 3. Multimedia File Analysis (Images, Audio, Video)
- **Goal:** Validate file format/integrity and uncover hidden data.
- **Typical Checks:**
  - Verify format compliance and integrity (ExifTool, MediaInfo).
  - Search for steganographic content (stegsolve, zsteg, steghide).
  - Detect hidden payloads, scripts, or polyglot files.
  - Look for EXIF anomalies, LSB encoding, or embedded malicious code.
  - Identify phishing‚Äërelated elements (e.g., QR codes).
- **Tools & Links:**
  - **ExifTool:** https://exiftool.org/
  - **MediaInfo:** https://mediaarea.net/en/MediaInfo
  - **Oletools:** https://github.com/decalage2/oletools

---

### 4. Android Package (APK) Analysis
- **Process:** Decompile the APK to inspect its inner components.
- **Key Artifacts to Review:**
  - `AndroidManifest.xml` ‚Äì permissions, services, components.
  - Hard‚Äëcoded IPs, URLs, keys, or credentials.
  - Security settings (2FA, linked apps, privacy configurations).
- **Tools & Links:**
  - **JADX** (decompiler): https://github.com/skylot/jadx
  - **Apktool** (resource & manifest extraction): https://github.com/iBotPeaches/Apktool
  - **Frida** (runtime instrumentation) ‚Äì no link provided.
  - **PhoneRescue** (iOS data recovery): https://www.iphone-data-recovery.com/
  - **iMazing** (device backup/extraction): https://imazing.com/

---

### 5. WhatsApp & Snap‚ÄëRelated Evidence
- **Account Overview:** Retrieve phone number, profile name, status.
- **Chat Analysis:** Examine individual and group messages, timestamps, and attached media.
- **Media Review:** Scrutinize images, videos, documents for metadata and hidden content.
- **Group Involvement:** Identify groups the user participates in.
- **Deleted Content Recovery:** Use forensic tools to recover deleted snaps or messages where applicable.
- **Tools & Links:**
  - **WhatsAppWebScraper:** https://github.com/whatsappruby/whatsapp-web-scraper
  - **iMazing:** https://imazing.com/
  - **ForensicExplorer:** https://www.forensicexplorer.com/
  - **Cellebrite:** https://www.cellebrite.com/ (used for both WhatsApp and Snap recovery)

---

### Overall Workflow Summary
1. **Preserve** the original data by imaging and hashing.
2. **Analyze** the file system and

1. Legal Framework for Search & Seizure : 

The legal authority to conduct search and seizure operations in cybercrime cases in the United Arab Emirates (UAE) is firmly rooted in Federal Decree-Law No. 35 of 2022 on the Criminal Procedure Law, and is further supported by Federal Decree-Law No. 34 of 2021 concerning Combating Rumors and Cybercrimes. These laws outline the boundaries, procedures, and safeguards that investigators must adhere to when handling digital evidence.

A. Search and Seizure under Federal Decree-Law No. 35 of 2022 (Criminal Procedure Law)



This law forms the backbone of procedural safeguards for criminal investigations, including those related to cyber offenses. Search and seizure powers are governed by several key provisions, primarily aimed at protecting individual rights while enabling effective law enforcement.

PART 4 : LEGAL ANALYSIS OF DIGITAL EVIDENCE

Legal Overview 

The analysis of digital evidence is a legally sensitive and technically demanding phase in cybercrime investigations. In the UAE, this process is regulated by Federal Decree-Law No. 34 of 2021 on Combatting Cybercrimes and Federal Decree-Law No. 35 of 2022 on the Law of Evidence, alongside procedural rules issued by enforcement agencies such as the Ministry of Interior (MOI), Dubai Police Cybercrime Unit, and TRA (Telecommunications and Digital Government Regulatory Authority).

The law mandates that digital evidence ‚Äî whether social media communications, IP logs, emails, or device contents ‚Äî must be handled in a forensically sound manner, with strict adherence to the chain of custody, authenticity protocols, and court-admissible formats. This section provides a legal-anchored guide to the technical analysis provided in the Part 3 of this Manual.

3.1 Social Media & Messaging Platforms

                Question: What is cyber law enforcement?
                Answer:
                
Answer:
**Cyber law enforcement** is the investigation and prosecution of cyber‚Äërelated offenses by authorized authorities (such as police, interior ministries, and regulatory bodies). It involves exercising powers‚Äîlike accessing, collecting, copying, preserving, and seizing digital data or systems‚Äîwhile following legal safeguards (chain of custody, forensic soundness, and court‚Äëadmissible procedures) established in statutes such as the UAE‚Äôs Federal Decree‚ÄëLaw No.‚ÄØ34‚ÄØ(2021) and No.‚ÄØ35‚ÄØ(2022).

A. Search and Seizure under Federal Decree-Law No. 35 of 2022 (Criminal Procedure Law)



This law forms the backbone of procedural safeguards for criminal investigations, including those related to cyber offenses. Search and seizure powers are governed by several key provisions, primarily aimed at protecting individual rights while enabling effective law enforcement.

üîπ Judicial Oversight and Warrant Requirement (Article 45 & 46)
Before any search or seizure is conducted, investigators must obtain authorization from the Public Prosecution or a competent judicial authority, except in exceptional circumstances (e.g., when a suspect is caught in the act‚Äîflagrante delicto).

Article 46(1) states that searches must be limited to the objects or persons specified in the judicial order. This ensures that digital searches do not become fishing expeditions.

Article 46(2) is especially critical for cybercrime investigations. It mandates that if the search involves electronic devices or digital systems, the process must:

Be done in a manner that does not affect the stored data

Preserve the integrity and authenticity of the information

Be documented accurately, including the method used to access or retrieve data

Answer:
**Cyber law‚Äëenforcement** refers to the set of investigative, procedural, and prosecutorial activities that authorities carry out to prevent, detect, and respond to crimes committed using computers, networks, or other digital technologies. In the United Arab Emirates (UAE) it is governed primarily by two federal decrees:

| Legal instrument | Core purpose | Key powers for cyber investigators |
|------------------|--------------|------------------------------------|
| **Federal Decree‚ÄëLaw No.‚ÄØ34 of‚ÄØ2021 (Cybercrime Law)** | Criminalises offences that involve electronic data, platforms, and devices; complements the Criminal Procedure Law. | ‚Ä¢ Access, collect, copy, and preserve digital data or systems.<br>‚Ä¢ Demand real‚Äëtime access or logs from service providers or custodians.<br>‚Ä¢ Do so without compromising data integrity or confidentiality unless a specific legal exception applies. |
| **Federal Decree‚ÄëLaw No.‚ÄØ35 of‚ÄØ2022 (Criminal Procedure Law)** | Provides the procedural safeguards for all criminal investigations, including cyber‚Äëcrimes. | ‚Ä¢ Requires a judicial or public‚Äëprosecution order (warrant) before any search or seizure, except in flagrante delicto or other urgent situations.<br>‚Ä¢ Limits the scope of a search to the objects or persons named in the order.<br>‚Ä¢ Mandates that electronic searches be performed in a way that does not alter the stored data, preserves authenticity, and is fully documented (method of access, tools used, chain‚Äëof‚Äëcustody, etc.). |

### Main Elements of Cyber Law‚ÄëEnforcement

1. **Legal Authority & Oversight**  
   - Investigators must obtain a warrant from a competent judicial authority or the Public Prosecution (Article‚ÄØ45‚Äë46 of Decree‚ÄëLaw‚ÄØ35).  
   - Exceptions exist for urgent actions (e.g., suspect caught in the act).

2. **Search & Seizure of Digital Evidence**  
   - Searches are limited to the specific devices, accounts, or data described in the order.  
   - Seizure must be performed without altering the original data (e.g., using forensic imaging, write‚Äëblockers).  

3. **Access & Preservation Powers**  
   - Under Article‚ÄØ48 of Decree‚ÄëLaw‚ÄØ34, authorities can compel service providers to supply real‚Äëtime logs, user data, or system access.  
   - Preservation includes making exact copies (forensic images) and securing the original media in a tamper‚Äëevident environment.

4. **Forensic Soundness**  
   - All actions must maintain the **integrity, authenticity, and admissibility** of evidence.  
   - Detailed documentation is required: who accessed the data, when, what tools were used, and the exact steps taken.  

5. **Confidentiality & Data Protection**  
   - While accessing data, investigators must respect confidentiality unless a specific legal provision authorises disclosure.  
   - Any breach of confidentiality without authority can constitute a separate offence.

6. **Roles & Responsibilities**  
   - **Law‚Äëenforcement agencies** (e.g., cybercrime units, police, specialized investigative bodies) conduct the technical collection and analysis.  
   - **Public Prosecution** reviews the legality of the actions and decides on charges.  
   - **Judiciary** provides oversight through warrants and reviews the admissibility of the evidence at trial.

7. **Prosecution & Admissibility**  
   - Evidence gathered in compliance with the above procedures is considered **forensically sound** and therefore admissible in UAE courts.  
   - Non‚Äëcompliant collection (e.g., altering data, lacking proper documentation) can lead to evidence being excluded.

### Summary

Cyber law‚Äëenforcement in the UAE is a structured process that blends **substantive cybercrime statutes** (Decree‚ÄëLaw‚ÄØ34) with **procedural safeguards** (Decree‚ÄëLaw‚ÄØ35). It empowers authorities to:

- Access, copy, and preserve digital

1. Legal Framework for Search & Seizure : 

The legal authority to conduct search and seizure operations in cybercrime cases in the United Arab Emirates (UAE) is firmly rooted in Federal Decree-Law No. 35 of 2022 on the Criminal Procedure Law, and is further supported by Federal Decree-Law No. 34 of 2021 concerning Combating Rumors and Cybercrimes. These laws outline the boundaries, procedures, and safeguards that investigators must adhere to when handling digital evidence.

PART 4 : LEGAL ANALYSIS OF DIGITAL EVIDENCE

Legal Overview 

The analysis of digital evidence is a legally sensitive and technically demanding phase in cybercrime investigations. In the UAE, this process is regulated by Federal Decree-Law No. 34 of 2021 on Combatting Cybercrimes and Federal Decree-Law No. 35 of 2022 on the Law of Evidence, alongside procedural rules issued by enforcement agencies such as the Ministry of Interior (MOI), Dubai Police Cybercrime Unit, and TRA (Telecommunications and Digital Government Regulatory Authority).

The law mandates that digital evidence ‚Äî whether social media communications, IP logs, emails, or device contents ‚Äî must be handled in a forensically sound manner, with strict adherence to the chain of custody, authenticity protocols, and court-admissible formats. This section provides a legal-anchored guide to the technical analysis provided in the Part 3 of this Manual.

This includes searching, seizing, accessing, collecting, copying, and preserving digital data or systems; demanding real‚Äëtime logs from service providers; and handling digital evidence in a forensically sound manner, all in accordance with the UAE‚Äôs Federal Decree‚ÄëLaw No.‚ÄØ34 of‚ÄØ2021 (Cybercrime Law) and Federal Decree‚ÄëLaw No.‚ÄØ35 of‚ÄØ2022 (Criminal Procedure Law).
